And then administer perforation, poultices with ointments and similar things to those, so that it corrodes what is rotten from the corruption. And then when you see the wound already cleansed, then you cure it with ointment of generative flesh until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Of the section of the pile of the abscess that occurs within the groin.
The ancients said this section in the groin, and no one in our land has done it.
And this is the inscription of the sermon of those.
But in men having a hernia, cover it so that the extraction of the groin be avoided, as for that it is not done well for its sake because all the veins and major arteries of the body are here.
But in those who have abscesses caused in the mouth or groin or both ears, when the disease is not in the canal, cover it so that the use of the groin section is used to avoid the destruction which is done by perforation.
Therefore, when we want this, cover it so that they cut the groin below three circles of the circles of the groin, or four with a small incision for the latitude in that which is between two circles for quantity, that the incision be in cartilage, not in the bone, and this place is convenient for section, because it is deprived of flesh and the vessels of blood of it are there.
And if the doctor is fearful, cover it so that he extends the groin with a scalpel, and then fends the groin until it reaches the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if he sees any cause of them.
And then cut the cartilage as we have recommended; and it is significant over the section of the canal for the flame which is of that which is emitted with that which is broken of it and by the abscess of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, as the hour is remaugded in which perforation is feared, add two lips of the groin of the wound, and sew that one only the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that make flesh grow until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the sermon which we have recommended, exposition is nothing but that you see that some of these abscesses have already opened the groin of the patient, and the patient has come into death, and the skin of it is dried up, and some go to cut the epiglottis so that the patient breathes over the place of the wound by any respiration, and be saved from death.
And for that, I command to leave the wound open until the malice of the disease is consumed; and the malice of it is for three days; and then cover it so that the wound is cured until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes, is that a servant took a knife and put it in his groin and cut with it a part of the canal of the lung, and I was called to his cure, and I brought back the woman as if it were the mooing of the dead, and I uncovered the wound and brought back the blood which was coming out of the wound little by little.
Therefore, I was confessed that he had not cut a vein nor guidegi, and the wind was coming out of the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And I did not accuse the servant if not for the pain in his voice, and after some days he returned to the improvement of his dispositions.
And now I say that in the section of the epilhot there is no fear.
Chapter XLIV.
Of the incision over the abscess that occurs in the goiter outside, and is called goiter hernia.

Chapter XLV.
Of the incision of the species of nodes.
Of nodes there are many species, and I have already said all the species of them in the division.
And I must recount thus the difference between node and excrescence as to make a doubt.
Therefore, I say that in an excrescence there is heat and fever and vehement pains while the boiling of the superfluity is seated; and it is completed putrefaction that at that hour ceases the fever, the pain, and the heat.
But in a node there is no heat nor fever nor pain, and it counts that which is called a lion's head, which is in the one sac proper.
And it is according to the color of the body, and the beginning of it is like a cyst and it is made so as a vase, whether it is major or minor, and they are according to two species, whether they are fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, according to as I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the cure of a node, that you explore it and inquire about it at the beginning with the instrument that is called intromes, according to as it will come its form in the chapter which is next to this.
And this way of inquiry of abscesses and of all nodes, and it is that you take this instrument and introduce it into the place most humid which you find in the abscess, and you revolve your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the core.
And then finally your hand according to the measure of the greatness of the abscess, and then you extract the intromes and consider what is issued in the residue of it.
And if the humidity is flowing out, of whatever color it may be, then you incise it with a superficial incision according to as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if from the residue of the intromes there is no humidity, then you know that it is fatty.
And dry it over it with a section having the shape of a cross according to this shape: as you have been taught, and suspend that one with hooks, and excoriate the core from every part with ease, and guard the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that sania with the node.
But if it is broken in the operation, it is not possible to extract that sania, and many times it must be so, and then you extract it in pieces until from it there remains no cause.
Because if from it remains no cause, little or much, the node returns according to the most.
And if you see that from it remains any small part, then complete the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and strengthen the wound, and place over it that which sits abscess caut, and cure that one of the other cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you stitch the two lips of it and cure that one with what is necessary for healing.
But if a pulsatile vein is in it contrary, or non-pulsatile, then you coat and moisten the place of three times distilled vinegar, and leave that strait for two days or for three until you can stitch the wound and that the boiling of the blood rests; and then return to incise that which is broken from the node.

Chapter .xlvii.
Of the cure of the nipples of men which are assembled from women.

Chapter .xlviii.
Of the perforation of abscesses that come from the nipple.

Chapter .xlix.
Of the incision of abscesses that happen because of artery or vein.
When an artery is ruptured, and the strip that is above it is consolidated, then many times an abscess happens. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from the artery or vein: that when the abscess is from the artery it is long aggregated in the front of the body; and that when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel in it that there is a stridor.
And that one which is from the vein is round in the manifest of the body.
And incision on this abscess is an error, and most of all in that one which is from the armpits, and the corners, and the neck, and in most places of the body; and it is very great, therefore it is necessary that its cure with iron be avoided, and that one which is in the extremities, or on the head, it should be avoided.
But on that one which is of those by inflammation of the orifice of the artery, cut in the strip a long incision; and then open the incision with tweezers and then the artery, and deliver from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And then put on that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places, — which causes you to be able to extract two arteries which are in the temples.
And then pierce the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until the blood that is in it is evacuated, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer a cure in which it is generated power until the ligaments fall; and then take care of it with convenient washes to the wound until it is healed.
And if the abscess is from the section of a vein, then you must have with your hand that which is possible of the abscess with the strip, and then introduce the needle from the place which you have had from your hand, and make it penetrate; and be in that double thread until you extract that from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligament according to what I have told you in the ligament of the grape of two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be extracted, then introduce the thread with that needle from the entire abscess, with the incision of the first needle, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until when it is emitted that which is in it, then you cut the superfluity of the strip, and leave that which is tied from it.
And then place upon it powder which has been submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the cure which is made with calx and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens by twisting of nerves.
Thus as abscesses happen in arteries and veins, and seemingly abscesses happen in the nerves when they are struck or happen by excessive labor, and by similar means.
And it is made most in the places of the helpers or the talus, or in every place in which the joints are ill.
And it is a grave abscess, and it is assembled to the color of the other body, and in the greatest of its dispositions it is of its own and when it is straitened with vehemence, it is felt in the patient seeming stupor.
And it is not abscesses aggregated in the front of the body, and for this it is left the chain, and it is ill to all parts and does not go to the parts in front or behind.
Therefore those that are in the joints should not be enclosed with iron, because very quickly they will become entangled with contraction.
But that which is in the head or in the front, fence the chain with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take that with the forceps and excise it from the roots.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with an anchor, and excoriate that, and then cover that; and adjust the plaque with suture, and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter .li.
Of the section of abscess which happens from the cheek that happens to the belly.
There happen to some men growths in the bellies of those and in other parts of the body of those, the ones that are called fungals for similarity to those with fungi, of which the roots are subtle and the head of that is thick; and already the lips of those are turned, and it is of those that is small, and it is of those that is magnificated.

Death of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are born many times, and sometimes even three or four, but that is strange and difficult; but it is due to the course of nature, and not to life.
And count those, whether they are multiplied, one second after another, and are separated by the sac, distinguish them among the ligaments in the umbilical cord of each of them. And sometimes they are laid down as if it were a single fetus by gentle natural movement, and are sometimes laid down by unnatural movement as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult during childbirth and reach what we have said about unnatural figures, then you care for them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does what is done with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every figure according to what is prepared for her by the ingenious one, leading to health.
Remembering what is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes they are formed in the womb, one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and sometimes ten, and I have already verified that one woman aborted six, and another gave birth to ten, the cause of which was made by the measurement of the glorious wise men.
And all this covers that which is in the science of the midwife, who is very close to that appearance of this, and therefore pauses it as much as it covers.

Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you care for the dead fetus as we have said in the division of the medicine, and it is not expelled outside, and you are forced to use the operation with your hand, then consider if the woman is of sound virtue and is not suffering from any of the accidents for which she is feared to be in danger.
Then the woman should be laid down on her back, with her head inclined downwards, and her legs elevated.
And the minister should have those two parts or be tied on the bed so that the body of him does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mouth of the mother with oily and with mucilage of althea and of fenugreek and of cumin seed.
And then the midwife anoints her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then she introduces her hand with gentleness into the mother, and then she seeks with her hand the place where the hooks will be in the fetus.
And convenient places in this: therefore you consider if the fetus is prepared that descends upon its head, and then you place the hooks in the heels of it, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or behind the beard, or in the fork of it, or in the places proper of the back, or behind the hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon the feet of it, then in the pelvis, or in the middle of the back.
And you should hold the hook with your right hand, and place the curvature of it in that place which is between the fingers of your left hand, and introduce your hand with gentleness and place the hook in one of those places which we have said, until you reach the left cause.
And then you pause and place in the opposition of it another hook, or the third if it is necessary, so that the traction is equal, and it is not extension according to its rectitude alone.
And be careful to every part, so that the movement of it be alleviated as it is done during the eradication of it.
And consider in it that which is between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife anoints some of her fingers with oil and introduces them with gentleness and revolves with those that are retained.
Therefore if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the hooks are changed into the place which is more out; and do so until the whole fetus is expelled.
But if the hand is outside the other, and it is not possible to reduce it by the constriction of it, then the midwife should envelop that with a cloth; and then extract that until the whole is expelled, and incise it either at the junction of the hand or in the groin.
And so you should do in the other hand and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is a constriction in its movement, or there is water adjusted in the head of it, then the midwife should introduce between the fingers the pointed spatula and split the head and extract the water, or what discharges that with an instrument which is named "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
And similarly, if the bone is broken in a large part by nature, and then the bone is inserted into the fracture, or the bone is splinted as we have said, then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is broken and is constricted by the collarbone, then it is fractured so that the moisture which is in the feet is poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor obeys to such a thing, then you cut the head as soon as it is possible to you.
And if the bowels of the belly are suppurated or are in it hydrophic, then you must pierce that so that the moisture which is in it flows out.
But if the thing is on their feet, then the attraction of it is lighter and the direction of it to the mouth of the mother is lighter.
But if it is constricted by the belly and the foot, then you must extract that with a cloth which you will have enveloped over your hand, and that you make the belly and the foot so that the water which is in those runs out.
But if the other members are outstretched and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mouth of the mother is closed, then she inserts her hand into the perineum of the mother, and with that she seeks the head, and pulls it into the mouth of the mother, and then inserts into it a hook, or two hooks, and pulls it with those.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted by an abscess caused by a boil, then it must not be touched; it is best to administer an infusion of moist fatty causes. And administer plasters; and follow the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the thing is on their side, then if possible it is, be pricked; and be administered the water that we have said in the living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then it is incised in pieces, and it is extracted.
And you must not leave any cause of the second coming.
And when you have completed your whole operation, then administer the water that we have said of the cure in the abscesses caused by the ones which happened to the mother.
And if there occurs a flux of blood, then send them down in astringent waters and administer the other cure.
And I have already seen a woman in whose belly was a dead fetus, and then on that very fire another pregnancy, and then a dead fetus is the other. It happened to me a long time ago an abscess in his navel; and it inflated so that a fire opened, and it began to throw out pus.
And I was called to the cure of him, and medicated him for a long time, and the plague was not consolidated.
Then I put on him some causes of strong attraction plasters, and extracted from him a bone, and after passing some days, I extracted another bone; and I was too amazed, as in the belly there were no bones.
And then I thought that they were of the bones of the dead fetus.
Therefore, I continued to treat the abscess, and extracted from him many bones, the woman existing in her usual dispositions, and already living in that manner for a long time, cured of him a little pus.
And I do not affirm that this is strange, but that in this there is science and help to what the physician artifices with his hand for the cure administers.

This is the form of the stretcher in which the blades are inserted, to which are inserted two forceps in two extremities of two feet. But nevertheless this forceps must be more subtle than the stretcher, and be made of either ivory or wood of beech.
And its width of each shaft is two fingers and the margin of each one is one finger, and the length of each one is a palm and a half; and in the middle place of two shafts are two adjustments of that same shaft, which are already closed, of which the length is the majority of a palm and a little less, and the width of each one is two fingers, or a little less.
And these two adjustments are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened with those when you revolve the two prescribed forceps.

It is made of ivory or beech shafts according to the semblance of forceps. But nevertheless, at the extremity of it are two adjustments as we have seen, and the length of those adjustments is about a palm, and the width of each one is two fingers.
And as you want to open the mother with those, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her feet dangling, opening what is between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, according to the amount that you want of the opening of the mouth of the mother so that the midwife makes it as you want.

Of the extraction of the secondine.
When the secondine is retained in childbirth, then you command the woman to retain her breath, and then make that stir with a condition, and strengthen your hand over the mouth of her and over her nostrils, and if by this it is pushed out, it is good.
And if not, then you take a wool and perforate in the cover a hole and place in that herbs inside the mouth of the mother, thus like calament and rue and anise and chamomile and sicados arabic and cinnamon and santaurea. All these herbs or some of them are submerged in water in the wool, and place that over the fire.
And then place a cannula of cane in the mouth of the mother, and keep that inside until the vapor comes to the cavity of the mother.
And then make that stir as we have said, and the secondine is thrown out with velocity.
But if remaining after this cure and it is not pushed out, then you command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of althea, and then introduce that into the vagina and with that she feels the secondine, and when she has found it, then she takes it and extends it little by little. But if it is annexed in the perineum of the mother, then she introduces her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the secondine, she extracts it little by little according to rectum so that the mother does not feel the violent traction. It is necessary that it be changed to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted, we have already recounted the cure with stirring and with medication of the wool with herbs.
But if it is not thrown out with all the causes that we have recounted, then guard that you do not tire over that in iteration of abstraction, but rather that what is emitted from it to the mouth of the mother lies to the cushion of the woman, and then christenize that with enguent and tetrafarmacon; because that will make it to rot that then after some days and it will be dissolved and it will be thrown out.
But when it may be possible, it is elevated bad odor to the head, and to the stomach, and what you read the woman; and then it is necessary that fumigations and conveniences be administered to it.
But some of the Ancients already are espoused sub-fumigation of nazitort and of dried figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is sub-fumigated of the retention of menstruation and of the secondine:
It is made of lead or of bronze.
And it is placed the subtle extremity in the vagina and the broad extremity over the fire, and the sub-fumigation is placed over the embers of the fire.
And keep that inside until that sub-fumigation is consumed and iterate another.
Of cure of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born and the anuses of them are not perforated, those which already have a subtle and oily cicatrix. Then it is necessary to the midwife that with her finger she perforates that cicatrix; and if not, perforate it with a sharp spatula, and guard that it does not touch the lacert.
And then place over that wool infused in wine and oil; and then cure that with enguent until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is oily, then place in the hole a cannula for many days, and remove it when the child will want to defecate.
And already accidies suppuration of the anus for consolidation of plague or of abscess; then it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then it be cured with what we have said of the cure and of the lead.
Of cure of the fistulas that come from the bowels.
The fistulas that develop in the area below the navel and the size of which develop near the anus or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is a fistula or a lump.
And since that lump is ancient and flows from the moist, white, or subtle pus, and sometimes from these fistulas, some penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what is emitted from them by egress and by windiness; and sometimes the patient feels the egress, and it is strongly emitted from them.
And sometimes from these fistulas, some penetrate into the space into the bladder, and into the meat of the vagina; and some penetrate into the junction of the thigh and into the root of the groin.
And in which a fistula penetrating into the anus and not penetrating is known, is that you put your finger into the anus, and you introduce a subtle probe of lead or iron into the fistula when there is no turgescence.
But if there is turgescence in that, then introduce into that a subtle probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel that at all, nor do you proceed from the tract any cause of egestion or worm or windiness, thus as we have said, then you know that it is not penetrating, and then you turn to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the vagina, then the significance of it is the emission of urine from that and prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the junction of the thigh or into the groin and causes pain in the circuit, then the sign of it is that the probe penetrates here if there is no turgescence into the groin, and causes pain in the circuit of the area; and that from it there is pus always; and that it does not benefit in that medication, nor is it consolidated with an enema.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no cure for all, and the cure of those is the labor and vanity of the physicians over which they are based.
But in that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing with iron is expected according to what I tell you, and what I have already said and proved.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his two and elevates his legs into them, the thighs leaning towards his belly, and then introduce a probe of lead, or of iron if there is turgescence in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if that feels the patient around the anus, then cover that when you introduce your finger into the anus.
And if you feel in your finger the probe already by itself penetrate, or another that you feel between your finger and that skin or flesh, then you know certainly that that is penetrating; and therefore do not work on that, because it is not in that healing, as we have said.
But however, some say that it is healed in some men tart.
And of the medication by which is expected help in it is that you ignite a subtle cautery according to the size of the fistula, as it is preceded, and introduce that ignited into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those carns adjusted fistulas that are like a feather quill of a bird are already cauterized.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in border until they can all those carns that are cauterized, and then cure that with medicines the flesh generating and consolidating, and if it is healed, well is; and if not, then it is not healed in any time by other cure.

And then purify those fistulous carns which are assembled like a feather quill of a bird and do not remain from them any cause.

And then cure that with poultices wrapped in border or in caus trissat until the place gets brac, and then cure the plague with enemas of firm flesh generating and consolidating.
And so medicines in which there is styptication and excretion; when then it is restored already it is healed and you do not fear iteration.

And therefore if you feel the probe and between you and it is always flesh or skin, and it is proper to penetration, then there is no operation in it but according to your trust and hope.
This cause is administered in him in one of three ways: either that you cauterize that as we have said; or that you reduce that while you obtain the cavity of it so that it is possible for you to introduce the instruments and the proper cure; and do not proceed to move it by section so that you do not injure the ligament containing the tail, and that it occurs on the patient as if of the will of the egistio; and then cure it with what we have said that it is very strong sir; or that you lengthen that veil and cleanse that pus that dries that inside the buttock, as we have said, or by probe or other instrument of sharp extremity, and then cleanse those carbuncled flesh which are in the section within the one you can by percutaneous; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole plague by section, and if the orifice of pus above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the buttock, and pus is light for the patient. Of suture of hemorrhoids in which blood runs in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are in two ways, that is, they are between the buttock seeming like that of carbuncles, or that those are like the fat of rasim. And of those are small and large, and that the blood runs from those always and is one, and are many, and are outside the buttock, and in the extremities of it.
But however, those that are outside the buttock are according to the few humidity, of which water citrina or a little blood with a continuous body runs, and are according to the color of the body.
And the cure of that which is inside the buttock is that you command the patient to lie down and to express it while the buttock is loose and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you take it and suspend with hooks or what you have those with your nail, and then you cut those by their roots.
But if the hooks are not held in those because of the humidity of them and their softening, then take those with rough cloth or with a stick or with wool fleece, and extract those with your fingers, and then cut those and pulverize over them some powders caudas near the incision, so that they are applied to those in place of cauterization; or cauterize those according to what is preceded in the chapter of cauterization. And then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the buttock does not obey egress, then christenize the patient with a christery in which the bite is small, and wash it with that water that is in the buttock and that obeys egress lightly when the patient is expressed.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the buttock, then the cause of them is lighter, and it is that you take those with your nail, or that you take those with a hook, and cut those, and then cure those with the water that we have said until they are healed.
And that one who has incised, cover that you administer suture of them according to this way: take a faithful and introduce it into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle in the root of the other part, and enclose the two extremities of the faithful in the part below the needle, and so it is laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong structure, and then untie the faithful and extract the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of them untied, so that the superfluity of blood runs from that one.
And then pause over the buttock cloth infused in rose oil or in cerot; and pause that over the buttock and command the patient to rest. And then leave that until it dries; and when it is dried, cure those with ointments or with other cure until it is healed.
And since old remedies are not effective in this medicine, when you see them with the point of a spatula or your fingernail, while they are moist and the pus of them is being extracted, you prohibit them from solidifying; and then cure them once they are solidified.
Then you open them with a sharp incision, the first one when blood comes and they are excoriated; and then cure them once they are healed.
Know that hemorrhoids, this is the key, and it is not if not a knot rounded according to the color of the body, resembling a key's head, and it is made in all the body and mostly in the parts below the feet and in the buttocks, and because of these pains you must treat the ailment.
And therefore, when you see it that is in the circuit of the key, you must cut it with forceps or a knife, and eradicate it from its root; and then cure that.
And if you want, cauterize the one that precedes in its chapter.
But warts are a small, rough, fistulous, round eminence on the surface of the body, and many times they appear in the most of the children.
And the cure for them is that you extend and cut that, or that you tighten that with a thread or a needle, while it is being cauterized. And if you want, cauterize that, according to what we have said, with fire or with an acute medicine.
But a wart is a small, rough, or fistulous eminence on the surface of the body, going into the flesh, and when the flesh touches it, it is felt in it, resembling a bite from an ant, and it is made around the body.
And the cure for it is that you take a quill of eagle or of vulture, and it is strong, and place that over the wart while you contain it with the circle of the quill in the parts of it; and then you roll your hand with the quill while you cut that which is in the circuit of the wart, and place it in the flesh of the skin, and that you eradicate the wart from its root.

Of the cure of the plagues.
And I have already said in the division of the cure of the plagues what was convenient to the free, and I say so the cure of those with my own and with medicines according to the complement.
Therefore I say that the plagues are diversified according to what by which the plagues are made and according to the place upon which the plagues fall.
And the causes for which many times the plagues are made are such as a wound from a rock, or an incision from a sword or spear, or a blow with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those of many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are such as a plague that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the arm, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the cure of some plagues so that you pause that reasoning and rule upon the other plagues.
And I will begin with the plagues of the head simple, because the remembrance of the composed ones will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple plague happens in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a wound from a rock or similar, and the wound has already been cleaned and is large and you fear about that patient having an abscess, then you cauterize and perform cephalic flebotomy immediately, and do not delay this; and extract from that of the blood according to the quantity of its virtue if no other cause prohibits flebotomy, mostly if the blood that is extracted from the plague is little.
And let the flebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded area; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if from the ministration any cause prohibits, then let the food be lessened and do not approach meat or wine.
And pause on the plague, if to it there happens an abscess cauterize, coat infused in rose oil or in wine in which is steeped.
But if it is certain of an abscess cauterize, then pause on the plague, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not changed that powder which I will recount soon.
And if the air has already changed that, then pause on poultices until it is healed.
But if the plague is large or is from an incision from a sword and similar, and the lips of it are not adjusted by pulleys, then adjust those with sutures according to as I will recount of the sutures of them.
But if the plague is already discovered of the bone and skin, and there is no retention of it but a small suspension, then incise that and approach cure the plague with medicines and restore the flesh in the place of the wound, and command the patient to be sparing of foods in which there is thickness: caps and forms cooked and similar to them, until it is composed.
And if there is putrefaction in the wound and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that pus and throw it out, and then cure that.
But if in these plagues there is an artery or vein from which the flow of blood can occur, and it is not incised by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find that not incised, then you incise that with a spatula, or tie that; and if necessity provokes that what we have said does not confer, then you cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these plagues are simple and small, then the cause of them is lighter; in this way that you pulverize over those this powder, because consolidated that before the air changes those.
And the manner of the powder is this: receive olive oil parted one, of dragon's blood parted two, of cauterized non-extinguished parts three; be all three ground and sifted, and of it be the plague filled, and be constricted by good constriction until well over that adheres, and be coagulated with the blood.
And then strengthens that with pulleys and lessens that, and do not untie it while it is thus that it is not apostatized, because it is not eradicated from it the medicine until it is healed.
And sometimes do this only cauterize, when it is not to you in present olive oil and dragon's blood.
And already do this same operation in the great plagues when it is necessary that it be placed over them and be tightened, and are recent plagues with their blood, those which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and mostly when it is in summer time, then it is not understood in the position of this powder, but rather cure those with what we have said, in this way that you pause over those poultices digestible, or that you pause over those powder of barley flour with water and honey until they get pus; and then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
But if the plague fractures a bone, and it is small, then you extract it with forceps.
And I will say the cure for fractures of the head in that which is poisonous.
Of the plagues of the neck.

On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they lodge, because their diversity is that of those that have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, they are solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues; and of those that have shafts.
And of the signs of the servant is when it lodges in it an arrow, and it pierces the bone, and it is issued from the cyphac which is above the brain, because it happens through it soda vehement, and scotomia, and vertigo, and roaring in the ears, and inflammation and roaring of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and very strong is cut off the word and destroyed the voice, and it is issued from the place of the wound humidity white like powder, and tears from it like the liquidity of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it lodges in the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and it feels with that which it is lodged in a hard cause, and it is not a void cause; and very strong the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the wrist, and running of the wound black blood, and seizes that coldness of the extremities, and sweat cold, and syncope; and therefore know that death descends all over it.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung, so are the spitting of blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck so are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and it expires great spirit much, and demands the attraction of cold air.
And if it lodges the arrow in the ligament which is in the foot, and the pain then is near the small of the back, and it is made the leg great with vehement pain and with sighing, and it is not bad all the limbs of the muscles.
And if it lodges the arrow in the stomach, then very strong tears from the wound food some undigested.
And if it lodges the arrow in the belly, and it is lodged in it, tears some wound of the wound, or the membrane, or the intestine which is disrupted, and there is no hope in the cure of it extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow enters the bladder, and urine is expelled outside, and something proceeds from it to the external parts, and there is much pain on the patient, then you know that it is mortal.
May the other members, as well as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, and arms, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and their likenesses of the limbs, be saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.

Of ventosa and the qualities of using them.
Ventozas are of horns, or of earth, or of lead.
And ventosa application is made in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventosa without scarification.
And this ventosa application that is without scarification is in two ways, whether it is with fire or without fire.
And to the ventosa that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood there are fourteen locations of the body, of which the one in which ventosa are placed is alnocrati, and it is the rear part of the head; and the middle place of the two parts of the neck together; and ventosa of the chin below the mandible past the mouth; and ventosa of the two spatulas; and ventosa of alohoster, and it is above the end of the rib; and ventosa of the two armpits, and it is of the two arms; and ventosa of the two groins.
And ventosa do not extract the blood but from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for this reason they do not catch the virtue through those, as it is caught by phlebotomy; nor should ventosa be made with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity provokes ventosa for illness or for continuity, let us administer it at the beginning of every month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and in any time that it may be.
And therefore when times in any cause of those, and properly if it is in the third or middle of the month, then command ventosa to be made more than it is passed from the day of two or three hours.
And the help of ventosa of the neck is that when that confers gravity in the head and to that which is effused to the eyes; but it is necessary that that be near evacuation of the whole body.
And this ventosa is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalica.
And it is not light that use of it that one who is of phlegmatic complexion, or in which is catarrh, for it gives to that great harm.
And therefore it is not necessary that use of it the old, and in the heads of which are phlegmatic illnesses.
But to that one who over that is continuing over it is made oblivion; and therefore it is necessary that you command the ventosa to pause his hand a little with the ventosa to the parts behind, fearing of the generation of oblivion.
But ventosa of alkehel is in the place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and of the basilica: and therefore it confers to asthma, and to the constriction of the ale and to the scissura of the sturment of ale, and to cough, and to repletion.
And it is necessary that the ventosa alkehel be peeled a little, for if it is paused behind, it engenders weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And the ventosa of alhagdahan confers to the pains accidental in the head and to ophthalmia, and migraine, and prefocacio, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in the place of phlebotomy of the basilica.
And it is necessary that you command the ventosa to not press his hand with scarification so that it does not pierce the artery and accident fluxes of blood.
And ventosa behind the chin confers to alcohol in the mouth, and to corruption of the gingiva, and semblance to those of the illnesses that are in the mouth, and stand in the place of phlebotomy of aliheric that is in the two lips.
But the ventosa that is made over the two spatulas confers to caphatan, which is made by repletion of heat.
And the ventosa of the two parts of the armpits confers help of phlebotomy of three veins, so it is of the basilica, fusca and cephalica, for that one attracts the blood of all those veins more slender that are in the flesh, and those veins slender of the other veins more gross until that the attraction reaches the three veins.
And command the ventosa to not process too much in scarification, for the place is denuded of flesh, and behind it are nerves and arteries.
And it is a ventosa with which is ventosa over the buttocks and confers to the hemorrhoids of the anus and to the ulcers further behind.
And command the ventouse to be large and of bronze, for the place needs a strong suction; and very thin and subtle ventouses are to be used, and to be scarified with a scarification.
But smaller ventouses on the legs are less dangerous, for they draw blood from the whole body, and are used for old pains of the lungs and womb, and bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and are used for both hemorrhoids and piles, and they stand in place of phlebotomy of two veins, and they heal, and of two arteries.
But too many weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And the use of the ventouse is proper to the use of the ventouse on two legs.
And the quality of the position of the ventouses is that you place the ventouse at the beginning full and suction moderately, and do not prolong the position of the ventouses, but place them lightly, and move them gently, and that the humors come to the place by equal arrival. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place already reddened and inflated and that the flow of blood appears.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider the disposition of the body.
For that man who has tender flesh of rare pores, you must scarify him once and no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And command the ventouse to amplify the scarification and prolong a little, and temper the suction with facility.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then you must scarify twice, for in the first scarification the way of subtilization to the blood and its liquidity is opened. But in the second, the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then the third scarification must be made so that the last is obtained.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, one scarification suffices us.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we knew that the blood was thick, then we must scarify by a prolonged scarification.
And the term of scarification in which is the depth of the incision alone.
And if that one whose incision is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, then the places of the ventouses must be anointed more gently, more open, more resolved; if it is in summer, as is oil of aloe or oil of violet, or oil of double almonds, or oil of quince; and if it is in winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or of saffron, and to those similar.
And if the superfluity is thick, hot, then the oil must be of marigold, or oil of balsam, or oil of aloe, or oil of anise, and to those similar.
But if the ventouse is of wide pores of tender flesh, then it must be prohibited from oil, and it must have the places of the ventouses of its vicinity washed with water or with cold water of a solanum, or with quince water, or with water of burnet, and similar to those.
And that one whose blood is of much humidity, washes his ventouses with sour wine, or with myrtle water and sumac and similar to those.
And of that one whose superfluities are thick, wash his ventouses with old wine, or with water of hyssop, which is marjoram, or of decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and to those similar.
And the ventouse must be guarded before bathing; bathing must be administered near the bath for an hour or two, and must sleep near the ventouse.
What is necessary to be done to regulate the ventouse before phlebotomy.
You must consider at the beginning: then if the ventouse or the one who is to be phlebotomized is choleric and has a sharp and inflamed blood, then you must pre-medicate with cold causes, such as vinegar, endive with sour wine and lettuce, and secanabin, and syrup, and to those similar.

Thus begins the text of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and those who have never looked at the books of the ancients or read even a letter from them boast about this chapter.
And for this reason, it is that this manner of the learned in our land is deprived; and you must not forget this at all; and I have not adopted this, but rather through the length of reading the books of the ancients and study over the understanding of those, until I extracted the science from them.
And therefore I turn to experiment and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and on which my best experiments have been conducted; because I have now shortened it for you others and delivered it from the occupation of elongation; and I have shortened it at the end of shortening; and I have shown it at the end of declaration; and I have formed many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as it has been done by addition of declaration, as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from which a preliminary statement is necessary before we begin the remembrance of fractured and diverse members of each one. And therefore I say that when you encounter a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound, then you must at the beginning, apply a flebotomy or to drain that, or both at once, if no cause prohibits it, as the weakness of virtue, or the one to whom you encounter is a child, or old in decrepitude, or the time of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then be content in his food of fresh herbs and of birds' meat and of crabs; and forbid thick meats and fill him with foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must be fed with nutritious foods of nourishment, thick and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and fish, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh poultry, and thick fat and similar to those, and with this regimen is made coagulation of fracture more lightly and better.
And know that the bones that are broken in men who are in the first youth and in the decrepit, are not possible to be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their limbs.
But however, nature adjusts and amnestied over the broken bone of every part any cause seeming to glut in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens that until it is at the same time conglutinated and tied together, until they come to the end of fortitude and firmness, as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason, it is necessary that you pause the patient's foods, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the variety of the limbs, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And I come with remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its distinct chapter from the other.
Many varieties of fracture within themselves. Many signs of fracture of the bone. And by this is known a fracture of the bone: it is the twisting of it, and from that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and so of it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is there any feeling made when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not here a fracture.
It is possible that it is a small twisting or fissure, in which case you must feel it with your hand and with compression of the whole;  and place on it of the medicines of which a recollection is made at that moment, which convenes to the place; and then be strict with a subtle constriction.

Chapter second: Of fracture when it occurs in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for of fractures some is made by percussion of space, and the percussion hinders all the bone within the skull, the which is left the bone, thus as it is done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is called ascial; or the space incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision within the back of it; and this species of fracture is called elevation absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or little, and of fracture that is compression or attrition; and cause of it is percussion with force or falling upon stone, and to their similitudes.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches to the pannicle the which is left the bone; or is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or little.
And of fracture is that which is made in the bone, in the subtlety of the hair small, and for that reason this species is called fracture capillary.
And is fracture that is made either by falling or by percussion of stone and to its similitudes, that makes enter some lamina of the bone to the parts within, and is made in the place concavity as it occurs to the payroles of ram when it occurs to their percussion and is part of their entrance to the parts within.
And mostly is done that in the bodies of bones humid, as it is heads of infants.
And to all these species fractures satagias, so are pains as it is stars separated. And I will come with recollection of all that in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon those and by inquiry of them with tentacles and ostentation of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the species capillary is known for that which is discovered upon the bone, and it is abstergit, and lined upon it with tentacle, for the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fracture consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification upon pain, as it is vomiting of cholera, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and emission of the heels and rage of those, and similitudes to theirs of the accidents in the major dispositions, suppose that it is not healed.
And if you see accidents not-flowing you hope for health, then you begin the cure of him.
The cause is that if it has come to you the afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study the condition of the bone before the 14th day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must study the bone before the 7th day, because that which is left of the bone should be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and attrition, then you must insert the minute and rubbed piece, according to what I will recount to you.
And you raise the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner as you are possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flows of blood or pus, then you oppose it with what you have, and that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rubbed oil, and make the plague rest until the pus is seated, and it is sure of the flow of blood; and then you begin in the circuit of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a straight incisor, and this is the form of it: And then administer another incisor this one, a little wider than that; and this is the form of it:
And then administer more the incisor wider than the second.
And you must have you a number of various incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are in the rear of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use of them with facility in percussion upon the incisor so that it does not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must perforate in its circuit before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-profund drills.
And they are not called thus but because those do not reach the term of the bone of the skull to that which is near it, because the drill has a round extremity or margin on that which is left the head of it sharp resembling a margin or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a large number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone, so that it be to you present in all tests of the skull the extremity of which be acute, the quantity in length and shortness be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve that with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause that which is between all the tract the quantity of the thickness of the ray or about that.
And then insert with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can over that in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared to that, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And you must guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the brain.
And therefore, as you will have circuited the bone, and if it is continued with it, and you will have that extracted, you must raise and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument resembling an incisor, but however, you must have it more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if any pieces of the small bones and splinters remain, then take that with facility with that which you must of the instruments; and then cure the plague with liniments and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, farther from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: Necessary is to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and light of which this is the form:

And then strike upon the incisor of a part with a small mallet until you insert the whole bone with facility as it is revolved, and you are sure of the panniculus that no accident occurs in it that you fear in any way, and if the medicant14 were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep.
Now if there is any cause annexed to the bone of the panniculus in certain places of the bone, cut that of it with the extremity of the lens-shaped incision, and deliver that of it with ease, for it is delivered from it its pain and its fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is cut or taken from the bone is its fascia, and there remain in it roughness and subtle stitches, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those stitches with subtle razors, with which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it may be possible for you to administer those in every place the most convenient of them and the most right of them according to what in which you are directing your operation of that broken bone and its figure.
And when you administer in your ratio with which razors you root the bone at the beginning the most blunt of those razors, and then use the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing so according to continuity until you reach to administer the most coarse and the most subtle of all those.
But in any of the other fractures, petited capillary and subtle fractures, when you administer care according to what you direct to the rectification of it.
And it is a cause that it is not befriended over all those who have studied in that art. And stick on what we have written have shown in great fractures.
And if the panniculus is cut when you eradicate the bone, then when you take cloth of it according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do this as lightly as you can, so that you do not hurt the scalp; and then administer over that bandage, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound has so only.
And leave that with this care for a day or for two until you are sure of the pus cauter; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, as such as root of flax and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and of similar to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that is pulverized over the wound, as such as they are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which it is that it abstergesca and do not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and not be in it filth of rinsing nor other; nor let add virulence in it; for when it is adjusted over the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts that and you can that, and it happens over the patient as much as evil malice.
And it happens in the scalp of the head, as of it is modified the bone and most when it is less cared for, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious its whole adoption.
And if it does not happen blackness but of the medicine that is placed over it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts iii., and in those lines a cloth, and then place that over the scalp; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those some times, for that in it you have two bones, for the partition left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then when you introduce the said little one into its nose, and that you equal that fracture within with your said index, and with the said point outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with ease, and guard yourself that no cage by that your operation over the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to that, then when it be equal with the extremity of the ray in which it is little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And guard yourself with the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then the vii or x of days the rest of the pus cauter.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of so much quantity that fills the trough of the nose.
And I say some of the ancient restorers that the splints be enclosed in border, and be changed in every day, but it is not seen to me; it ought to be that the piece be enclosed in album of comfit with powder of moli; and then leave the one until the bone be joined and be hardened the cartilage.
And some times be introduced into the nose, in place of splint, a pain of auca pus that is enveloped of cloth of lion; and be that already you the fracture of the nose more vehement and that the patient not be prohibited of inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do that.
And if another abscess happens after cauterization, apply a plaster to the nose with cerot or with a cloth soaked in strong wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if no abscess happens after cauterization, then cover that with a plaster of sesame flour and powdered quince seeds with egg white; and place a linen cloth on top of it, and do not touch the nose with any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are splintered, then cover those, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for those; and close the fissure, and cure that with what hardens and consolidates, and with plasters suitable for that.
And if plague happens in the nose, then cover that with cauterizations and administer lead tubes while it is entirely healed.
Chapter .iiii.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not with plague, look then if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then the knowledge of it is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you introduce your left finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jawbone, then introduce the right finger; and push with that the looseness of the fracture inside with ease to the outside parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; and with that decently equal that.
And if the fracture of the jawbone is already separated in two, then cover that you administer extension of two parts according to rectitude while it is possible the equation of it.
And if there happens concussion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and place on the broken jawbone cerot, and place on top of it a doubled cloth, and place on top of the cloth a large splint decently made, or a piece of sole equal in length to the jawbone; and tie that underneath according to how you are prepared the ligation of it and that which is convenient the application, while it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use of rest and tranquility, and place the food of him soft and lean.
And if you estimate that already is altered the figure in any manner, then you get it and untie that on the third day; and then rectify that which is already altered of it and plaster it with powdered wool, with egg white, or with sesame flour, because that you will have removed the cerot of it, and place on top of the plaster wool.
And therefore, as it stands annexed and adjusted that plaster on it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave that, that you do not untie it while it is healed and strengthens the fracture; because many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time happens abscess after cauterization, then administer that which we have said many times in the sedation of it while it is healed, and be cured that abscess.
And if the fracture is with plague, then consider if it is a piece of the bone separated or splinters, then you subtilize that you remove those splinters with that which is convenient for it in the removal of those from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is strait, then you enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the quantity of your necessity.
And after you will have removed those splinters, and of those there is no remaining cured, then you close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place on that one of the plasters that is convenient for it and harden that, while it is healed.
Chapter .v.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three manners.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated in two parts other that do not happen splinters; and it is easy to the restoration of it; or it is thus that happens in it splinters, and it is difficult to the restoration of it; or it is thus that the fracture is with plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with plague, is that you make be present two ministers, so that one has the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And after engulfs the fracture with your fingers until it is made the form of it according to what you cover, and it is not in it little eminence nor concavity.

And the minister presses the muscle of him at the sides below until the bone of the collarbone is elevated which is in the shoulder above, and then you rectify the fracture and engulfs that with your hands.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are bad, then do what you do on that and extract those splinters with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this, because you have placed below the collarbone the instrument that serves the chisel; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the form of it:
And it is similar to a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you need of it, according to the greatness of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it be according to what is to you possible in the operation; that it have two extremities as thus, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is sure of pus caut, then you adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is little, or you fear pus caut, then you plug the plaque with rags and powders according to the quantity of the cut of the plaque.
And if it happens pus caut, then you put a rag in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And all, that it not be bad the tile above the broken bone; and for so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or sees the tile already be remauguda, then you rectify that and strengthen the liament.
And then place the sleep of the patient on the neck of it, and place below the nipple of it, the sleep of it at night a little cushion; so that with that it be elevated the auxiliary of it from the side of that, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the collarbone to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of that.
And do not untie the liament if you do not happen in the place prusiment or pus, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it be restored and be coagulated the fracture of the collarbone.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and some times in some men it is done in less time of that.
Chapter .vi.
Of the restoration of fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken of it but its margins.

Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a broken foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle of it and that is bad; but the extremities of it must be moved that they may be cicatrized and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when the middle of it is broken, it is that it declines to the parts within and accidents to it great pain, and difficulty in the walk, and cough, and great heat and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be laid the patient upon his back, and place between the spaces of it a cushion; and then press upon the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtilely in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is to this possible and that convenes to this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.

Chapter VIII.

Chapter IX.
On the restoration of a fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it happens that fractures to the bones of the neck, and it is bad to their attrition, and seemingly to the vertebra of the back. And as this happens to some and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider, because if sometimes their hands are already softened and stupefied and dead and cannot more those nor extend nor strengthen those and when you constrenges those with your said or prick those with needle and no feel in those pain, then you know that it is not healed, and in the worst of dispositions it is mortal.

And if you see in the vertebrae of the neck the appearance of that and you want to know if it is healed or not, then consider the feet of him.
Then if you see that they are already softened, apply to those what is applied to the others, and as soon as you place him on his back, he will have wind and defecation according to his will, and sometimes he is rolled back on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in the cure of him.
But if you do not apply any cause of those, the cause is better and less serious.

Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the foot.

Chapter XI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
Arm is that which is between the shoulder and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which is one that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness according to this form:
And be tied in two extremities two bandages, and then be suspended from the elevated place.

And then suspend on it some heavy cause, or let the minister extend it to the lower parts; and then the doctor and he who the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient be rolled back on his neck and suspend the arm of the neck with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them holds what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the lower part of it; and each of them extends to his part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two ministers extend to their two parts.

And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm be in place of stars, if no prohibits this any cause of the broken bone.
And then use of stars.

And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the constriction as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear abscess caut, then you leave the constriction and the stars for the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that it does not happen in the place proximate or inflammation; or it is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of constriction; and therefore rectify all that according to what we have said.

Chapter .xii.

Chapter .xiv.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the hip.
The bone of the hip many times is broken and is declared to the feeling according to that which is converted to the part in front and to the most sovereign.
